<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Renewable energy</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Energy that is collected from renewable resources</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For the journal, see <a href="/wiki/Renewable_Energy_(journal)" title="Renewable Energy (journal)"><i>Renewable Energy</i> (journal)</a>.</div>

<p><b>Renewable energy</b> is energy that is collected from <a href="/wiki/Renewable_resource" title="Renewable resource">renewable resources</a> that are naturally replenished on a <a href="/wiki/Orders_of_magnitude_(time)" title="Orders of magnitude (time)">human timescale</a>. It includes sources such as <a href="/wiki/Solar_power" title="Solar power">sunlight</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">wind</a>, the movement of <a href="/wiki/Hydropower" title="Hydropower">water</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_energy" title="Geothermal energy">geothermal heat</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> Although most renewable energy sources are <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_energy" title="Sustainable energy">sustainable</a>, some are not. For example, some <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">biomass</a> sources are considered unsustainable at current rates of <a href="/wiki/Exploitation_of_natural_resources" title="Exploitation of natural resources">exploitation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CarbonBrief1_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-CarbonBrief1-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SciAm_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-SciAm-4">[4]</a></sup> Renewable energy often provides energy for <a href="/wiki/Electricity_generation" title="Electricity generation">electricity generation</a> to a grid, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_heating" title="Space heating">air</a> and <a href="/wiki/Water_heating" title="Water heating">water heating</a>/<a href="/wiki/Air_conditioning" title="Air conditioning">cooling</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Stand-alone_power_system" title="Stand-alone power system">stand-alone power systems</a>. 
</p><p>From 2011 to 2021, renewable energy has grown from 20% to 28% of global electricity supply. Fossil energy shrunk from 68% to 62%, and nuclear from 12% to 10%. The share of hydropower decreased from 16% to 15% while power from sun and wind increased from 2% to 10%. Biomass and geothermal energy grew from 2% to 3%. There are 3,146 gigawatts installed in 135 countries, while 156 countries have laws regulating the renewable energy sector.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEREN21_Renewables_Global_Status_Report2021_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEREN21_Renewables_Global_Status_Report2021-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>Globally there are over 10 million jobs associated with the renewable energy industries, with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_photovoltaics" title="Solar photovoltaics">solar photovoltaics</a> being the largest renewable employer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Renewable energy systems are rapidly becoming more efficient and cheaper and their share of total energy consumption is increasing,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> with a large majority of worldwide newly installed electricity capacity being renewable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> In most countries, <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_system" title="Photovoltaic system">photovoltaic solar</a> or <a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">onshore wind</a> are the cheapest new-build electricity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEA_Renewables_2020_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-IEA_Renewables_2020-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many nations around the world already have renewable energy contributing more than 20% of their total energy supply, with <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_renewable_electricity_production" title="List of countries by renewable electricity production">some generating over half their electricity from renewables</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> A few countries generate all their electricity using renewable energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> National renewable energy markets are projected to continue to grow strongly in the 2020s and beyond.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEREN21_Renewables_Global_Futures_Report2017_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEREN21_Renewables_Global_Futures_Report2017-13">[13]</a></sup> Studies have shown that a global transition to <a href="/wiki/100%25_renewable_energy" title="100% renewable energy">100% renewable energy</a> across all sectors – power, heat, transport and desalination – is feasible and economically viable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:32_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-:32-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-:8-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Renewable energy resources exist over wide geographical areas, in contrast to fossil fuels, which are concentrated in a limited number of countries. Deployment of renewable energy and <a href="/wiki/Efficient_energy_use" title="Efficient energy use">energy efficiency</a> technologies is resulting in significant <a href="/wiki/Energy_security_and_renewable_technology" title="Energy security and renewable technology">energy security</a>, <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_mitigation" title="Climate change mitigation">climate change mitigation</a>, and economic benefits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-International_Energy_Agency_2012_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-International_Energy_Agency_2012-17">[17]</a></sup> However renewables are being hindered by hundreds of billions of dollars of <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel_subsidies" title="Fossil fuel subsidies">fossil fuel subsidies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> In international <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Public_opinion_surveys" title="Public opinion surveys">public opinion surveys</a> there is strong support for renewables such as solar power and wind power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNEP_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNEP-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> But the <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">International Energy Agency</a> said in 2021 that to reach <a href="/wiki/Carbon_neutrality" title="Carbon neutrality">net zero carbon emissions</a> more effort is needed to increase renewables, and called for generation to increase by about 12% a year to 2030.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Renewable energy technology projects are typically large-scale, but they are also suited to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rural" title="Rural">rural</a> and remote areas and <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_in_developing_countries" title="Renewable energy in developing countries">developing countries</a>, where energy is often crucial in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_development_(humanity)" title="Human development (humanity)">human development</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> As most of the renewable energy technologies provide electricity, renewable energy is often deployed together with further <a href="/wiki/Electrification" title="Electrification">electrification</a>, which has several benefits: electricity can <a href="/wiki/Heat_pump" title="Heat pump">move heat</a> or objects efficiently, and is clean at the point of consumption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> In addition, electrification with renewable energy is more efficient and therefore leads to significant reductions in primary energy requirements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> In 2021, China accounted for almost half of the global increase in renewable electricity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886046785">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style><div class="toclimit-3"><div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Outline_of_solar_energy" title="Outline of solar energy">Outline of solar energy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lists_of_renewable_energy_topics" title="Lists of renewable energy topics">Lists of renewable energy topics</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Global_Energy_Consumption.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="791" data-file-width="960" decoding="async" height="247" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Global_Energy_Consumption.svg/300px-Global_Energy_Consumption.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Global_Energy_Consumption.svg/450px-Global_Energy_Consumption.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Global_Energy_Consumption.svg/600px-Global_Energy_Consumption.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Global_Energy_Consumption.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coal, oil, and natural gas remain the primary global energy sources even as renewables have begun rapidly increasing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:T%C3%BBranor_PlanetSolar_Rabat.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3199" data-file-width="4830" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/T%C3%BBranor_PlanetSolar_Rabat.JPG/220px-T%C3%BBranor_PlanetSolar_Rabat.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/T%C3%BBranor_PlanetSolar_Rabat.JPG/330px-T%C3%BBranor_PlanetSolar_Rabat.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/T%C3%BBranor_PlanetSolar_Rabat.JPG/440px-T%C3%BBranor_PlanetSolar_Rabat.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:T%C3%BBranor_PlanetSolar_Rabat.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/T%C3%BBranor_PlanetSolar" title="Tûranor PlanetSolar">PlanetSolar</a>, the world's largest solar-powered boat and the first ever solar electric vehicle to circumnavigate the globe (in 2012)</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">Definition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Definition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_energy" title="Sustainable energy">Sustainable energy</a></div>
<p>Renewable energy flows involve natural phenomena such as <a href="/wiki/Sunlight" title="Sunlight">sunlight</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wind" title="Wind">wind</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tide" title="Tide">tides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">plant growth</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_heating" title="Geothermal heating">geothermal heat</a>, as the <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">International Energy Agency</a> explains:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Renewable energy is derived from natural processes that are replenished constantly. In its various forms, it derives directly from the sun, or from heat generated deep within the earth. Included in the definition is electricity and heat generated from solar, wind, ocean, <a href="/wiki/Hydropower" title="Hydropower">hydropower</a>, biomass, geothermal resources, and biofuels and hydrogen derived from renewable resources.</p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Drivers_and_benefits">Drivers and benefits</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Drivers and benefits">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Renewable energy stands in contrast to <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuels</a>, which are being used far more quickly than they are being replenished. Renewable energy resources and significant opportunities for <a href="/wiki/Efficient_energy_use" title="Efficient energy use">energy efficiency</a> exist over wide geographical areas, in contrast to other energy sources, which are concentrated in a limited number of countries. Rapid deployment of renewable energy and energy efficiency, and technological diversification of energy sources, would result in significant <a href="/wiki/Energy_security_and_renewable_technology" title="Energy security and renewable technology">energy security</a> and economic benefits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-International_Energy_Agency_2012_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-International_Energy_Agency_2012-17">[17]</a></sup> Solar and wind power have got much cheaper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> In some cases it will be cheaper to transition to these sources as opposed to continuing to use the current, inefficient, fossil fuels. It would also reduce environmental <a href="/wiki/Pollution" title="Pollution">pollution</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">air pollution</a> caused by the burning of fossil fuels, and improve public health, reduce premature mortalities due to pollution and save associated health costs that could amount to trillions of dollars annually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Multiple analyses of decarbonization strategies have found that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Value_of_a_statistical_life" title="Value of a statistical life">quantified health benefits</a> can significantly offset the costs of implementing these strategies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gallagher_et_al_2020_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gallagher_et_al_2020-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">Climate change</a> concerns, coupled with the continuing fall in the costs of some renewable energy equipment, such as wind turbines and solar panels, are driving increased use of renewables.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNEP_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-UNEP-19">[19]</a></sup> New government spending, regulation and policies helped the industry weather the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%9308" title="Financial crisis of 2007–08">global financial crisis</a> better than many other sectors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-obama_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-obama-35">[35]</a></sup> As of 2019<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, however, according to the <a href="/wiki/International_Renewable_Energy_Agency" title="International Renewable Energy Agency">International Renewable Energy Agency</a>, renewables overall share in the energy mix (including power, heat and transport) needs to grow six times faster, in order to keep the rise in average global temperatures "well below" 2.0 °C (3.6 °F) during the present century, compared to pre-industrial levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scale">Scale</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Scale">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy#Market_and_industry_trends" title="Renewable energy">Renewable energy § Market and industry trends</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_commercialization" title="Renewable energy commercialization">Renewable energy commercialization</a></div>
<p>A household's solar panels, and batteries if they have them, can often either be used for just that household or if connected to an electrical grid can be aggregated with millions of others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Over 44 million households use <a href="/wiki/Biogas" title="Biogas">biogas</a> made in household-scale digesters for <a href="/wiki/Lighting" title="Lighting">lighting</a> and/or <a href="/wiki/Cooking" title="Cooking">cooking</a>, and more than 166 million households rely on a new generation of more-efficient biomass cookstoves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEREN21_Renewables_Global_Status_Report201114_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEREN21_Renewables_Global_Status_Report201114-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="do people really still use biogas for lighting? (June 2022)">needs update</span></a></i>]</sup> According to the research, a nation must reach a certain point in its growth before it can take use of more renewable energy. In our words, its addition changed how crucial input factors (labor and capital) connect to one another, lowering their overall <a href="/wiki/Elasticity_(economics)" title="Elasticity (economics)">elasticity</a> and increasing the apparent economies of scale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> United Nations' eighth Secretary-General <a href="/wiki/Ban_Ki-moon" title="Ban Ki-moon">Ban Ki-moon</a> has said that renewable energy has the ability to lift the poorest nations to new levels of prosperity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-renewableenergyworld1_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-renewableenergyworld1-40">[40]</a></sup> At the national level, at least 30 nations around the world already have renewable energy contributing more than 20% of energy supply.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Although many countries have various policy targets for longer-term shares of renewable energy these tend to be only for the power sector,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> including a 40% target of all electricity generated for the European Union by 2030.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses">Uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Renewable energy often displaces conventional fuels in four areas: <a href="/wiki/Electricity_generation" title="Electricity generation">electricity generation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_water_heating" title="Solar water heating">hot water</a>/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_heating" title="Space heating">space heating</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transportation" title="Transportation">transportation</a>, and rural (off-grid) energy services.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEREN21_Renewables_Global_Status_Report2010_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEREN21_Renewables_Global_Status_Report2010-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Power_generation">Power generation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Power generation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>More than a quarter of electricity is generated from renewables as of 2021.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Heating_and_cooling">Heating and cooling</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Heating and cooling">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Solar_water_heating" title="Solar water heating">Solar water heating</a> makes an important contribution to <a href="/wiki/Renewable_heat" title="Renewable heat">renewable heat</a> in many countries, most notably in China, which now has 70% of the global total (180  GWth). Most of these systems are installed on multi-family apartment buildings<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> and meet a portion of the hot water needs of an estimated 50–60 million households in China. Worldwide, total installed solar water heating systems meet a portion of the water heating needs of over 70 million households. In Sweden, national use of biomass energy has surpassed that of oil. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heat_pumps" title="Heat pumps">Heat pumps</a> provide both heating and cooling, and also flatten the electric demand curve and are thus an increasing priority<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-energy.gov_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-energy.gov-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Net_Zero_Foundation_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Net_Zero_Foundation-48">[48]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Renewable_thermal_energy" title="Renewable thermal energy">Renewable thermal energy</a> is also growing rapidly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> About 10% of heating and cooling energy is from renewables.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Transportation">Transportation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Transportation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Soybeanbus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="322" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Soybeanbus.jpg/220px-Soybeanbus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Soybeanbus.jpg/330px-Soybeanbus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Soybeanbus.jpg/440px-Soybeanbus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Soybeanbus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A bus fueled by <a href="/wiki/Biodiesel" title="Biodiesel">biodiesel</a></div></div></div>
<p>One of the efforts to decarbonize transportation is the increased use of <a href="/wiki/Electric_vehicle" title="Electric vehicle">electric vehicles</a> (EVs).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
Despite that and the use of <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuels</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Aviation_biofuel" title="Aviation biofuel">biojet</a>, less than 4% of transport energy is from renewables.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-51">[51]</a></sup> Occasionally <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen_fuel_cells" title="Hydrogen fuel cells">hydrogen fuel cells</a> are used for heavy transport.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mainstream_technologies">Mainstream technologies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Mainstream technologies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Solar_energy">Solar energy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Solar energy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Solar_energy" title="Solar energy">Solar energy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Solar_power" title="Solar power">Solar power</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2020-05-20,_Sentinel-2A_L1C,_True_color_(1).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5670" data-file-width="9930" decoding="async" height="126" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/2020-05-20%2C_Sentinel-2A_L1C%2C_True_color_%281%29.jpg/220px-2020-05-20%2C_Sentinel-2A_L1C%2C_True_color_%281%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/2020-05-20%2C_Sentinel-2A_L1C%2C_True_color_%281%29.jpg/330px-2020-05-20%2C_Sentinel-2A_L1C%2C_True_color_%281%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/2020-05-20%2C_Sentinel-2A_L1C%2C_True_color_%281%29.jpg/440px-2020-05-20%2C_Sentinel-2A_L1C%2C_True_color_%281%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2020-05-20,_Sentinel-2A_L1C,_True_color_(1).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Satellite image of the <a href="/wiki/Bhadla_Solar_Park" title="Bhadla Solar Park">Bhadla Solar Park</a> in <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, it is the largest Solar Park in the world</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:World_GHI_Solar-resource-map_GlobalSolarAtlas_World-Bank-Esmap-Solargis.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1122" data-file-width="1889" decoding="async" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/World_GHI_Solar-resource-map_GlobalSolarAtlas_World-Bank-Esmap-Solargis.png/330px-World_GHI_Solar-resource-map_GlobalSolarAtlas_World-Bank-Esmap-Solargis.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/World_GHI_Solar-resource-map_GlobalSolarAtlas_World-Bank-Esmap-Solargis.png/495px-World_GHI_Solar-resource-map_GlobalSolarAtlas_World-Bank-Esmap-Solargis.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/World_GHI_Solar-resource-map_GlobalSolarAtlas_World-Bank-Esmap-Solargis.png/660px-World_GHI_Solar-resource-map_GlobalSolarAtlas_World-Bank-Esmap-Solargis.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:World_GHI_Solar-resource-map_GlobalSolarAtlas_World-Bank-Esmap-Solargis.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Global map of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Horizontal_irradiation" title="Horizontal irradiation">horizontal irradiation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></div></div></div>

<p><a href="/wiki/Solar_energy" title="Solar energy">Solar energy</a>, radiant <a href="/wiki/Light" title="Light">light</a> and <a href="/wiki/Heat" title="Heat">heat</a> from the sun, is harnessed using a range of ever-evolving technologies such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_heating" title="Solar heating">solar heating</a>, <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaics" title="Photovoltaics">photovoltaics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Concentrated_solar_power" title="Concentrated solar power">concentrated solar power</a> (CSP), <a href="/wiki/Concentrator_photovoltaics" title="Concentrator photovoltaics">concentrator photovoltaics</a> (CPV), <a href="/wiki/Solar_architecture" title="Solar architecture">solar architecture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Artificial_photosynthesis" title="Artificial photosynthesis">artificial photosynthesis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Most new renewable energy is solar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> Solar technologies are broadly characterized as either <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Passive_solar" title="Passive solar">passive solar</a> or active solar depending on the way they capture, convert, and distribute solar energy. Passive solar techniques include orienting a building to the Sun, selecting materials with favorable <a href="/wiki/Thermal_mass" title="Thermal mass">thermal mass</a> or light dispersing properties, and designing spaces that <a href="/wiki/Ventilation_(architecture)" title="Ventilation (architecture)">naturally circulate air</a>. Active solar technologies encompass <a href="/wiki/Solar_thermal_energy" title="Solar thermal energy">solar thermal energy</a>, using <a href="/wiki/Solar_thermal_collector" title="Solar thermal collector">solar collectors</a> for heating, and solar power, converting <a href="/wiki/Sunlight" title="Sunlight">sunlight</a> into <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a> either directly using <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaics" title="Photovoltaics">photovoltaics</a> (PV), or indirectly using <a href="/wiki/Concentrated_solar_power" title="Concentrated solar power">concentrated solar power</a> (CSP).
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_system" title="Photovoltaic system">photovoltaic system</a> converts light into electrical <a href="/wiki/Direct_current" title="Direct current">direct current</a> (DC) by taking advantage of the <a href="/wiki/Photoelectric_effect" title="Photoelectric effect">photoelectric effect</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Solar PV has turned into a multi-billion, <a href="/wiki/Growth_of_photovoltaics" title="Growth of photovoltaics">fast-growing</a> industry, continues to improve its cost-effectiveness, and has the most potential of any renewable technologies together with CSP.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> Concentrated solar power (CSP) systems use lenses or mirrors and tracking systems to focus a large area of sunlight into a small beam. Commercial concentrated solar power plants were first developed in the 1980s. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dish_stirling" title="Dish stirling">CSP-Stirling</a> has by far the highest efficiency among all solar energy technologies.
</p><p>In 2011, the <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">International Energy Agency</a> said that "the development of affordable, inexhaustible and clean solar energy technologies will have huge longer-term benefits. It will increase countries' energy security through reliance on an indigenous, inexhaustible and mostly import-independent resource, enhance <a href="/wiki/Sustainability" title="Sustainability">sustainability</a>, reduce pollution, lower the costs of mitigating <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>, and keep fossil fuel prices lower than otherwise. These advantages are global. Hence the additional costs of the incentives for early deployment should be considered learning investments; they must be wisely spent and need to be widely shared".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-58">[58]</a></sup> Solar power accounts for 505 GW annually, which is about 2% of the world's electricity. Solar energy can be harnessed anywhere that receives sunlight; however, the amount of solar energy that can be harnessed for electricity generation is influenced by <a href="/wiki/Weather" title="Weather">weather conditions</a>, geographic location and time of day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> According to chapter 6 of the IPCC 2022 climate mitigation report, the global potential of direct solar energy far exceeds that of any other renewable energy resource. It is well beyond the total amount of energy needed in order to support mitigation over the current century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>  Australia has the largest proportion of solar electricity in the world, supplying 9.9% of the country's electrical demand in 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> More than 30 per cent of Australian households now have rooftop solar PV, with a combined capacity exceeding 11 GW.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wind_power">Wind power</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Wind power">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">Wind power</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wind_energy_generation_by_region,_OWID.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="850" decoding="async" height="247" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Wind_energy_generation_by_region%2C_OWID.svg/350px-Wind_energy_generation_by_region%2C_OWID.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Wind_energy_generation_by_region%2C_OWID.svg/525px-Wind_energy_generation_by_region%2C_OWID.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Wind_energy_generation_by_region%2C_OWID.svg/700px-Wind_energy_generation_by_region%2C_OWID.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wind_energy_generation_by_region,_OWID.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wind energy generation by region over time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Global_Map_of_Wind_Power_Density_Potential.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1490" data-file-width="2500" decoding="async" height="197" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Global_Map_of_Wind_Power_Density_Potential.png/330px-Global_Map_of_Wind_Power_Density_Potential.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Global_Map_of_Wind_Power_Density_Potential.png/495px-Global_Map_of_Wind_Power_Density_Potential.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Global_Map_of_Wind_Power_Density_Potential.png/660px-Global_Map_of_Wind_Power_Density_Potential.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Global_Map_of_Wind_Power_Density_Potential.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Global map of wind power density potential.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></div></div></div>

</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Global electricity power generation capacity annual growth rate</td>
<td>13% (2012-2021)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Share of global electricity generation</td>
<td>5% (2018)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEA_Electricity_2020_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-IEA_Electricity_2020-56">[56]</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Levelized cost per megawatt hour</td>
<td>Land-based wind: USD 30.165 (2019)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENREL_ATB2021Land-Based_Wind_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENREL_ATB2021Land-Based_Wind-72">[72]</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Primary technology</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Wind_turbine" title="Wind turbine">Wind turbine</a>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Other energy applications</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Windmill" title="Windmill">Windmill</a>, <a href="/wiki/Windpump" title="Windpump">windpump</a>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Air flow can be used to run <a href="/wiki/Wind_turbine" title="Wind turbine">wind turbines</a>. Modern utility-scale wind turbines range from around 600 kW to 9 MW of rated power. The power available from the wind is a function of the cube of the wind speed, so as wind speed increases, power output increases up to the maximum output for the particular turbine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EWEA_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-EWEA-73">[73]</a></sup> Areas where winds are stronger and more constant, such as offshore and high-<a href="/wiki/Altitude" title="Altitude">altitude</a> sites, are preferred locations for wind farms. Typically, <a href="/wiki/Capacity_factor" title="Capacity factor">full load hours</a> of wind turbines vary between 16 and 57 percent annually but might be higher in particularly favorable offshore sites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>Wind-generated electricity met nearly 4% of global electricity demand in 2015, with nearly 63 GW of new wind power capacity installed. Wind energy was the leading source of new capacity in Europe, the US and Canada, and the second largest in China. In Denmark, wind energy met more than 40% of its electricity demand while Ireland, Portugal and Spain each met nearly 20%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>Globally, the long-term technical potential of wind energy is believed to be five times total current global energy production, or 40 times current electricity demand, assuming all practical barriers needed were overcome. This would require wind turbines to be installed over large areas, particularly in areas of higher wind resources, such as offshore. As offshore wind speeds average ~90% greater than that of land, so offshore resources can contribute substantially more energy than land-stationed turbines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydropower">Hydropower</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Hydropower">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">Hydroelectricity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydropower" title="Hydropower">Hydropower</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ThreeGorgesDam-China2009.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1768" data-file-width="2847" decoding="async" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/ThreeGorgesDam-China2009.jpg/220px-ThreeGorgesDam-China2009.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/ThreeGorgesDam-China2009.jpg/330px-ThreeGorgesDam-China2009.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/ThreeGorgesDam-China2009.jpg/440px-ThreeGorgesDam-China2009.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ThreeGorgesDam-China2009.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Three_Gorges_Dam" title="Three Gorges Dam">Three Gorges Dam</a> on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yangtze_River" title="Yangtze River">Yangtze River</a> in China</div></div></div>
<table class="wikitable">
<tbody><tr>
<td>Global electricity power generation capacity</td>
<td>1,230.0 GW (2021)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Global electricity power generation capacity annual growth rate</td>
<td>2.5% (2012-2021)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Share of global electricity generation</td>
<td>16% (2018)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEA_Electricity_2020_56-2"><a href="#cite_note-IEA_Electricity_2020-56">[56]</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Levelized cost per megawatt hour</td>
<td>USD 65.581 (2019)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENREL_ATB2021Hydropower_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENREL_ATB2021Hydropower-79">[79]</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Primary technology</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity#Generating_methods" title="Hydroelectricity">Dam</a>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Other energy applications</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Pumped-storage_hydroelectricity" title="Pumped-storage hydroelectricity">Pumped storage</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydropower#Mechanical_power" title="Hydropower">mechanical power</a>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Since water is about 800 times <a href="/wiki/Density_of_air" title="Density of air">denser than air</a>, even a slow flowing stream of water, or moderate sea <a href="/wiki/Swell_(ocean)" title="Swell (ocean)">swell</a>, can yield considerable amounts of energy. There are many forms of water energy:
</p>
<ul><li>Historically, hydroelectric power came from constructing large hydroelectric dams and reservoirs, which are still popular in <a href="/wiki/Developing_country" title="Developing country">developing countries</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/List_of_largest_hydroelectric_power_stations" title="List of largest hydroelectric power stations">largest</a> of them are the <a href="/wiki/Three_Gorges_Dam" title="Three Gorges Dam">Three Gorges Dam</a> (2003) in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Itaipu_Dam" title="Itaipu Dam">Itaipu Dam</a> (1984) built by Brazil and Paraguay.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Small_hydro" title="Small hydro">Small hydro</a> systems are hydroelectric power installations that typically produce up to <span class="nowrap">50 MW</span> of power. They are often used on small rivers or as a low-impact development on larger rivers. China is the largest producer of hydroelectricity in the world and has more than 45,000 small hydro installations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Run-of-the-river_hydroelectricity" title="Run-of-the-river hydroelectricity">Run-of-the-river hydroelectricity</a> plants derive energy from rivers without the creation of a large <a href="/wiki/Reservoir" title="Reservoir">reservoir</a>. The water is typically conveyed along the side of the river valley (using channels, pipes and/or tunnels) until it is high above the valley floor, whereupon it can be allowed to fall through a penstock to drive a turbine. A run-of-river plant may still produce a large amount of electricity, such as the <a href="/wiki/Chief_Joseph_Dam" title="Chief Joseph Dam">Chief Joseph Dam</a> on the Columbia River in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> However many run-of-the-river hydro power plants are <a href="/wiki/Micro_hydro" title="Micro hydro">micro hydro</a> or <a href="/wiki/Pico_hydro" title="Pico hydro">pico hydro</a> plants.</li></ul>
<p>Hydropower is produced in 150 countries, with the Asia-Pacific region generating 32 percent of global hydropower in 2010.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The date of the event predicted near this tag has passed. (June 2022)">needs update</span></a></i>]</sup> Of the top 50 countries by percentage of electricity generated from renewables, 46 are primarily hydroelectric.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> There are now three hydroelectricity stations larger than 10 GW: the <a href="/wiki/Three_Gorges_Dam" title="Three Gorges Dam">Three Gorges Dam</a> in China, <a href="/wiki/Itaipu_Dam" title="Itaipu Dam">Itaipu Dam</a> across the Brazil/Paraguay border, and <a href="/wiki/Guri_Dam" title="Guri Dam">Guri Dam</a> in Venezuela.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wi2012_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-wi2012-84">[84]</a></sup> Much hydropower is flexible, thus complementing wind and solar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Wave_power" title="Wave power">Wave power</a>, which captures the energy of ocean surface waves, and <a href="/wiki/Tidal_power" title="Tidal power">tidal power</a>, converting the energy of tides, are two forms of hydropower with future potential; however, they are not yet widely employed commercially.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>  A demonstration project operated by the <a href="/wiki/Ocean_Renewable_Power_Company" title="Ocean Renewable Power Company">Ocean Renewable Power Company</a> on the coast of <a href="/wiki/Maine" title="Maine">Maine</a>, and connected to the grid, harnesses tidal power from the <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Fundy" title="Bay of Fundy">Bay of Fundy</a>, location of the world's highest tidal flow. <a href="/wiki/Ocean_thermal_energy_conversion" title="Ocean thermal energy conversion">Ocean thermal energy conversion</a>, which uses the temperature difference between cooler deep and warmer surface waters, currently has no economic feasibility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bioenergy">Bioenergy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Bioenergy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">Biomass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biogas" title="Biogas">Biogas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">Biofuel</a></div>

data-file-height="519" data-file-width="531" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Metz_biomass_power_station.jpg/225px-Metz_biomass_power_station.jpg" <td>USD 118.908 (2019)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENREL_ATB2021Other_Technologies_(EIA)_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENREL_ATB2021Other_Technologies_(EIA)-91">[91]</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Primary technologies</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">Biomass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuel</a>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Other energy applications</td>
<td>Heating, cooking, transportation fuels
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Biomass is <a href="/wiki/Biomaterial" title="Biomaterial">biological material</a> derived from living, or recently living organisms. It most often refers to plants or plant-derived materials which are specifically called <a href="/wiki/Lignocellulosic_biomass" title="Lignocellulosic biomass">lignocellulosic biomass</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Biomass_Energy_Center_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Biomass_Energy_Center-92">[92]</a></sup> As an energy source, biomass can either be used directly via combustion to produce heat, or indirectly after converting it to various forms of <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuel</a>. Conversion of biomass to biofuel can be achieved by different methods which are broadly classified into: <i>thermal</i>, <i>chemical</i>, and <i>biochemical</i> methods. Wood was the largest biomass energy source as of 2012;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scheck2012_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Scheck2012-93">[93]</a></sup> examples include forest residues – such as dead trees, branches and <a href="/wiki/Tree_stump" title="Tree stump">tree stumps</a> –, yard clippings, wood chips and even <a href="/wiki/Municipal_solid_waste" title="Municipal solid waste">municipal solid waste</a>. In the second sense, biomass includes plant or animal matter that can be converted into fibers or other industrial <a href="/wiki/Chemical_industry" title="Chemical industry">chemicals</a>, including biofuels. Industrial biomass can be grown from numerous types of plants, including <a href="/wiki/Miscanthus" title="Miscanthus">miscanthus</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Switchgrass" title="Switchgrass">switchgrass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hemp" title="Hemp">hemp</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">corn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Populus" title="Populus">poplar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Willow" title="Willow">willow</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sorghum" title="Sorghum">sorghum</a>, sugarcane, <a href="/wiki/Bamboo" title="Bamboo">bamboo</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> and a variety of tree species, ranging from <a href="/wiki/Eucalyptus" title="Eucalyptus">eucalyptus</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_palm" title="Oil palm">oil palm</a> (<a href="/wiki/Palm_oil" title="Palm oil">palm oil</a>).
</p><p>Plant energy is produced by crops specifically grown for use as fuel that offer high biomass output per hectare with low input energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> The grain can be used for liquid transportation fuels while the straw can be burned to produce heat or electricity. Plant biomass can also be degraded from cellulose to <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a> through a series of chemical treatments, and the resulting sugar can then be used as a first-generation biofuel.
</p><p>Biomass can be converted to other usable forms of energy such as <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> gas<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> or transportation fuels such as <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a> and <a href="/wiki/Biodiesel" title="Biodiesel">biodiesel</a>. Rotting garbage, and agricultural and human waste, all release methane gas – also called <a href="/wiki/Landfill_gas" title="Landfill gas">landfill gas</a> or <a href="/wiki/Biogas" title="Biogas">biogas</a>. Crops, such as corn and sugarcane, can be fermented to produce the transportation fuel, ethanol. Biodiesel, another transportation fuel, can be produced from left-over food products such as vegetable oils and animal fats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> There is a great deal of research involving <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Algal_fuel" title="Algal fuel">algal fuel</a> or algae-derived biomass due to the fact that it is a non-food resource, grows around 20 times faster than other types of food crops, such as corn and soy, and can be grown almost anywhere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UllahAhmadSofia2014_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-UllahAhmadSofia2014-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ZhuLiHiltunen2018_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-ZhuLiHiltunen2018-99">[99]</a></sup> Once harvested, it can be fermented to produce biofuels such as ethanol, <a href="/wiki/Butanol" title="Butanol">butanol</a>, and methane, as well as biodiesel and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>. The biomass used for electricity generation varies by region. Forest by-products, such as wood residues, are common in the United States. Agricultural waste is common in <a href="/wiki/Mauritius" title="Mauritius">Mauritius</a> (sugar cane residue) and <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a> (rice husks). 
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">Biofuels</a> include a wide range of fuels which are derived from biomass. The term covers <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biofuels#Solid_biofuels" title="Biofuels">solid</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biofuels#Ethanol" title="Biofuels">liquid</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biofuels#Biogas" title="Biofuels">gaseous</a> fuels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Liquid biofuels include bioalcohols, such as bioethanol, and oils, such as biodiesel. Gaseous biofuels include <a href="/wiki/Biogas" title="Biogas">biogas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Landfill_gas" title="Landfill gas">landfill gas</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synthetic_gas" title="Synthetic gas">synthetic gas</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bioethanol" title="Bioethanol">Bioethanol</a> is an alcohol made by <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fermentation" title="Ethanol fermentation">fermenting</a> the sugar components of plant materials and it is made mostly from sugar and starch crops. These include maize, sugarcane and, more recently, <a href="/wiki/Sweet_sorghum" title="Sweet sorghum">sweet sorghum</a>. The latter crop is particularly suitable for growing in dryland conditions, and is being investigated by <a href="/wiki/International_Crops_Research_Institute_for_the_Semi-Arid_Tropics" title="International Crops Research Institute for the Semi-Arid Tropics">International Crops Research Institute for the Semi-Arid Tropics</a> for its potential to provide fuel, along with food and animal feed, in arid parts of Asia and Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p>With advanced technology being developed, cellulosic biomass, such as trees and grasses, are also used as feedstocks for ethanol production. Ethanol can be used as a fuel for vehicles in its pure form, but it is usually used as a gasoline additive to increase octane and improve vehicle emissions. Bioethanol is widely used in the <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel_in_the_United_States" title="Ethanol fuel in the United States">United States</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel_in_Brazil" title="Ethanol fuel in Brazil">Brazil</a>. The energy costs for producing bio-ethanol are almost equal to, the energy yields from bio-ethanol. However, according to the <a href="/wiki/European_Environment_Agency" title="European Environment Agency">European Environment Agency</a>, biofuels do not address global warming concerns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eea-biofuels_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-eea-biofuels-102">[102]</a></sup> Biodiesel is made from <a href="/wiki/Vegetable_oil" title="Vegetable oil">vegetable oils</a>, <a href="/wiki/Animal_fat" title="Animal fat">animal fats</a> or recycled greases. It can be used as a fuel for vehicles in its pure form, or more commonly as a diesel additive to reduce levels of particulates, carbon monoxide, and hydrocarbons from diesel-powered vehicles. Biodiesel is produced from oils or fats using <a href="/wiki/Transesterification" title="Transesterification">transesterification</a> and is the most common biofuel in Europe. Biofuels provided 2.7% of the world's transport fuel in 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEREN21_Renewables_Global_Status_Report201113–14_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEREN21_Renewables_Global_Status_Report201113–14-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The date of the event predicted near this tag has passed. (November 2021)">needs update</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Biomass, biogas and biofuels are burned to produce heat/power and in doing so harm the environment. Pollutants such as sulphurous oxides (SO<sub>x</sub>), <a href="/wiki/NOx" title="NOx">nitrous oxides</a> (NO<sub>x</sub>), and particulate matter (PM) are produced from the combustion of biomass. The World Health Organization estimates that 3.7 million prematurely died from outdoor air pollution in 2012 while indoor pollution from biomass burning effects over 3 billion people worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geothermal_energy">Geothermal energy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Geothermal energy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_energy" title="Geothermal energy">Geothermal energy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_power" title="Geothermal power">Geothermal power</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Renewable_thermal_energy" title="Renewable thermal energy">Renewable thermal energy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NesjavellirPowerPlant_edit2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="2400" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/NesjavellirPowerPlant_edit2.jpg/220px-NesjavellirPowerPlant_edit2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/NesjavellirPowerPlant_edit2.jpg/330px-NesjavellirPowerPlant_edit2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/NesjavellirPowerPlant_edit2.jpg/440px-NesjavellirPowerPlant_edit2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NesjavellirPowerPlant_edit2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Steam rising from the <a href="/wiki/Nesjavellir_Geothermal_Power_Station" title="Nesjavellir Geothermal Power Station">Nesjavellir Geothermal Power Station</a> in Iceland</div></div></div>

<td>Dry steam, flash steam, and binary cycle power stations
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Other energy applications</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Geothermal_heating" title="Geothermal heating">Heating</a>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>High temperature geothermal energy is from <a href="/wiki/Thermal_energy" title="Thermal energy">thermal energy</a> generated and stored in the Earth. Thermal energy is the energy that determines the <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">temperature</a> of matter. Earth's geothermal energy originates from the original formation of the planet and from <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive decay</a> of minerals (in currently uncertain<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dye_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dye-109">[109]</a></sup> but possibly roughly equal<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gando_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gando-110">[110]</a></sup> proportions). The <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_gradient" title="Geothermal gradient">geothermal gradient</a>, which is the difference in temperature between the core of the planet and its surface, drives a continuous conduction of thermal energy in the form of heat from the core to the surface. The adjective <i>geothermal</i> originates from the Greek roots <i>geo</i>, meaning earth, and <i>thermos</i>, meaning heat.
</p><p>The heat that is used for geothermal energy can be from deep within the Earth, all the way down to Earth's core – 4,000 miles (6,400 km) down. At the core, temperatures may reach over 9,000 °F (5,000 °C). Heat conducts from the core to the surrounding rock. Extremely high temperature and pressure cause some rock to melt, which is commonly known as magma. Magma convects upward since it is lighter than the solid rock. This magma then heats rock and water in the crust, sometimes up to 700 °F (371 °C).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p><p>Low temperature geothermal<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-energy.gov_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-energy.gov-47">[47]</a></sup> refers to the use of the outer crust of the Earth as a <a href="/wiki/Thermal_battery" title="Thermal battery">thermal battery</a> to facilitate <a href="/wiki/Renewable_thermal_energy" title="Renewable thermal energy">renewable thermal energy</a> for heating and cooling buildings, and other refrigeration and industrial uses. In this form of geothermal, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geothermal_heat_pump" title="Geothermal heat pump">geothermal heat pump</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ground-coupled_heat_exchanger" title="Ground-coupled heat exchanger">ground-coupled heat exchanger</a> are used together to move heat energy into the Earth (for cooling) and out of the Earth (for heating) on a varying seasonal basis. Low-temperature geothermal (generally referred to as "GHP"<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="I thought low temperature was just for heat not P (June 2022)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup>) is an increasingly important renewable technology because it both reduces total annual energy loads associated with heating and cooling, and it also flattens the electric demand curve eliminating the extreme summer and winter peak electric supply requirements. Thus low temperature geothermal/GHP is becoming an increasing national<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title='this is a global article - why "national" here? (June 2022)'>clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> priority with multiple tax credit support<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> and focus as part of the ongoing movement toward net zero energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Net_Zero_Foundation_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-Net_Zero_Foundation-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Emerging_technologies">Emerging technologies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Emerging technologies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>There are also other renewable energy technologies that are still under development, including <a href="/wiki/Cellulosic_ethanol" title="Cellulosic ethanol">cellulosic ethanol</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hot-dry-rock" title="Hot-dry-rock">hot-dry-rock</a> geothermal power, and <a href="/wiki/Marine_energy" title="Marine energy">marine energy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEA_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-IEA-113">[113]</a></sup> These technologies are not yet widely demonstrated or have limited commercialization. Many are on the horizon and may have potential comparable to other renewable energy technologies, but still depend on attracting sufficient attention and research, development and demonstration (RD&amp;D) funding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEA_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-IEA-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are numerous organizations within the academic, federal,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="not a US article - does this mean many governments or just a few rich ones? (June 2022)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and commercial sectors conducting large-scale advanced research in the field of renewable energy. This research spans several areas of focus across the renewable energy spectrum. Most of the research is targeted at improving efficiency and increasing overall energy yields.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup>
Multiple government supported research organizations have focused on renewable energy in recent years. Two of the most prominent of these labs are <a href="/wiki/Sandia_National_Laboratories" title="Sandia National Laboratories">Sandia National Laboratories</a> and the <a href="/wiki/National_Renewable_Energy_Laboratory" title="National Renewable Energy Laboratory">National Renewable Energy Laboratory</a> (NREL), both of which are funded by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Energy" title="United States Department of Energy">United States Department of Energy</a> and supported by various corporate partners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Enhanced_geothermal_system">Enhanced geothermal system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Enhanced geothermal system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:EGS_diagram.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1052" data-file-width="744" decoding="async" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/EGS_diagram.svg/170px-EGS_diagram.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/EGS_diagram.svg/255px-EGS_diagram.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/EGS_diagram.svg/340px-EGS_diagram.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:EGS_diagram.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Enhanced_geothermal_system" title="Enhanced geothermal system">Enhanced geothermal system</a> <i>(see <a href="/wiki/File:EGS_diagram.svg" title="File:EGS diagram.svg">file description</a> for details)</i></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enhanced_geothermal_systems" title="Enhanced geothermal systems">Enhanced geothermal systems</a></div>
<p>Enhanced geothermal systems (EGS) are a new type of geothermal power technology that does not require natural convective hydrothermal resources. The vast majority of geothermal energy within drilling reach is in dry and non-porous rock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Duchane_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Duchane-116">[116]</a></sup> EGS technologies "enhance" and/or create geothermal resources in this "hot dry rock (HDR)" through <a href="/wiki/Hydraulic_fracturing" title="Hydraulic fracturing">hydraulic fracturing</a>. EGS and HDR technologies, such as hydrothermal geothermal, are expected to be baseload resources that produce power 24 hours a day like a fossil plant. Distinct from hydrothermal, HDR and EGS may be feasible anywhere in the world, depending on the economic limits of drill depth. Good locations are over deep <a href="/wiki/Granite" title="Granite">granite</a> covered by a thick (3–5 km) layer of insulating sediments which slow heat loss.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> There are HDR and EGS systems currently being developed and tested in France, Australia, Japan, Germany, the U.S., and Switzerland. The largest EGS project in the world is a 25 megawatt demonstration plant currently being developed in the Cooper Basin, Australia. The Cooper Basin has the potential to generate 5,000–10,000 MW.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marine_energy">Marine energy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Renewable_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Marine energy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rance_tidal_power_plant.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Rance_tidal_power_plant.JPG/220px-Rance_tidal_power_plant.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Rance_tidal_power_plant.JPG/330px-Rance_tidal_power_plant.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Rance_tidal_power_plant.JPG/440px-Rance_tidal_power_plant.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rance_tidal_power_plant.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Rance_Tidal_Power_Station" title="Rance Tidal Power Station">Rance Tidal Power Station</a>, France</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Marine_energy" title="Marine energy">Marine energy</a></div>
<p>Marine energy (also sometimes referred to as ocean energy) is the energy carried by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ocean_wave" title="Ocean wave">ocean waves</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tide" title="Tide">tides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Salinity" title="Salinity">salinity</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ocean_thermal_energy_conversion" title="Ocean thermal energy conversion">ocean temperature differences</a>. The movement of water in the world's oceans creates a vast store of <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic energy</a>, or energy in motion. This energy can be harnessed to generate electricity to power homes, transport and industries. The term marine energy encompasses <a href="/wiki/Wave_power" title="Wave power">wave power</a> – power from surface waves, marine current power - power from marine hydrokinetic streams (e.g., the Gulf Stream), and <a href="/wiki/Tidal_power" title="Tidal power">tidal power</a> – obtained from the kinetic energy of large bodies of moving water. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reverse_electrodialysis" title="Reverse electrodialysis">Reverse electrodialysis</a> (RED) is a technology for generating electricity by mixing fresh river water and salty sea water in large power cells designed for this purpose; as of 2016, it is being tested at a small scale (50 kW). <a href="/wiki/Offshore_wind_power" title="Offshore wind power">Offshore wind power</a> is not a form of marine energy, as wind power is derived from the <a href="/wiki/Wind" title="Wind">wind</a>, even if the <a href="/wiki/Wind_turbine" title="Wind turbine">wind turbines</a> are placed over water. The <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">oceans</a> have a tremendous amount of energy and are close to many if not most concentrated populations. Ocean energy has the potential of providing a substantial amount of new renewable energy around the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEA_20210511_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-IEA_20210511-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (May 2022)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>

<tr>
<td align="center">3.</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Annapolis_Royal_Generating_Station" title="Annapolis Royal Generating Station">Annapolis Royal Generating Station</a></td>
<td>Canada</td>
<td><span style="font-size:85%;"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1073938472" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//geohack.toolforge.org/geohack.php?pagename=Renewable_energy&amp;params=44_45_07_N_65_30_40_W_&amp;title=Annapolis+Royal+Generating+Station"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">44°45′07″N</span> <span class="longitude">65°30′40″W</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="vcard"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">44.75194°N 65.51111°W</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">44.75194; -65.51111</span></span><span style="display:none">﻿ (<span class="fn org">Annapolis Royal Generating Station</span>)</span></span></span></a></span></span></td>
